This geotechnical data report (GDR) presents the results of our field exploration 
and laboratory testing performed for the Kamehameha Highway Segment of the 
Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHCTCP) extending from Pearl City to 
Aiea in the Ewa area on the Island of Oahu, Hawaii. 
The information presented in this geotechnical data report is not intended to be 
sufficient for final project design and construction. A detailed, site-specific geotechnical 
investigation will need to be performed by the Project Geotechnical Engineer based on 
the contract requirements and at the actual project structure locations. 
If you have questions or need additional information, please contact our office.

SECTION 1.0 - PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
The Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project (HHCTCP) is a 20-mile 
elevated rail line (First Project) proposed for the Island of Oahu. This First Project would 
connect Kapolei in West Oahu with the urban core, e.g. Ala Moana Center. This 
Geotechnical Data Report presents the results of our field exploration and laboratory 
testing program performed for the approximately 3.9-mile long Kamehameha Highway 
Segment extending from Pearl City to Aiea in the District of Ewa, Project Station 770+00 
to Station 974+94.21, respectively. The project location and general vicinity are shown 
on the Project Location Map, Plate 1. 
The geotechnical study presented herein was based on the initial conceptual 
plans [circa 2008] for the guideway alignment to advance the Project design team 
efforts with preliminary design of the aerial guideway foundations being the primary 
focus. As such, it must be noted that the guideway alignment and substructure locations 
as depicted on the Preliminary Design Plans have undergone adjustments subsequent 
to the geotechnical work scope execution. 
This segment of the HHCTCP consists of a dual track, aerially supported 
guideway, two transit stations, and transit system appurtenant facilities and requires 
revisions to the existing infrastructure, including utilities associated with project 
execution. As currently planned, the top of rail for the guideway varies from almost 
29 feet to 56 feet above the existing ground. Based on the guideway section and 
bearing dimensions (10.5 feet) and a minimum of 2 feet below the existing ground 
embedment for foundations, the corresponding column heights will range from 20 feet to 
almost 50 feet. Spans between columns have been planned as being in the range of 
about 125 feet ± 25 feet based on studies completed for segmental construction. The 
several sections requiring spans exceeding 200 feet are designated special 
substructures and are envisioned as being balanced cantilever guideway construction.  
Consistent with the overall Project approach, it is the design team’s intent that 
the guideway be supported on single reinforced concrete columns supported by single, 
concentrically aligned drilled shaft foundations (substructure), such as a standard bent, 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 1 – PROJECT CONSIDERATIONS 
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 2 
Hawaii ● California 
to the extent practicable. However, given the constraints posed by the Kamehameha 
Highway urban corridor, such as space limitations imposed by existing infrastructure, it 
also is anticipated that straddle bents or eccentrically loaded substructure elements also 
may be required where standard bents are not feasible. 
As noted above, the widely spaced geotechnical investigation locations were 
selected based on conceptual level plan and profile information and consideration of the 
anticipated subsurface conditions. As guideway alignment designs progressed, 
additional explorations were completed in selected areas as evidenced by the 
numbering sequences. Spacing of substructures and locations of the supporting 
foundations will be the responsibility of the final designers. 
Appurtenant facilities for the aerial guideway in this segment consist of two 
stations (Pearlridge and Aloha Stadium) and five traction power substations (TPSS). As 
currently planned (please refer to February 2010 Preliminary Design Plans), both 
stations will be a center platform measuring 240 feet in length and supporting station 
platforms, walkways, and access facilities. 
Revisions to the existing infrastructure as evidenced by the Preliminary Design 
Plans will include utility protection and relocations, pavement overlays, new pavements, 
and nominal grade adjustments, which in some instance will require retaining walls of 
up to 6 feet in height. 
A summary of the field exploration program conducted for the Kamehameha 
Highway Segment of the HHCTCP is presented below. Detailed information and 
discussions for each specific work item are provided in the respective appendices of this 
geotechnical data report.

SECTION 2.0 - SCOPE OF WORK 
The purpose of our field exploration and laboratory testing program was to obtain 
an overview of the surface and subsurface conditions along the Kamehameha Highway 
Segment extending from Pearl City to Aiea for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit 
Corridor Project to advance the project design to the Preliminary Engineering level and 
cost estimation. The emphasis of the field exploration and laboratory testing program 
was focused on the foundation design of the aerial fixed guideway structures.  
Our work was performed in general accordance with the scopes of services 
outlined in our fee proposals dated December 17, 2007 and September 26, 2008, 
the Professional Services Subcontract with PB Americas, Inc. entered into on 
December 4, 2007, and Amendment 1 to the Subcontract dated May 2, 2008. In order 
to accomplish this, we conducted a field exploration and laboratory testing program 
consisting of the following tasks and efforts: 
1. 
Research and review of available in-house geologic and soils information 
in the project vicinity. A list of the references used in the preparation of this 
geotechnical data report is presented in the reference section.  
2. 
Preparation and submittal of a field exploration work plan for the 
subsurface exploration. 
3. 
Preparation and submittal of an accident prevention plan with activity 
hazard analysis in support of our field exploration activities. 
4. 
Coordination with the State of Hawaii – Department of Transportation, 
Highways Division to obtain the necessary excavation permits for the 
boring/seismic piezocone penetration testing (SCPTu) locations within the 
public right-of-way. A copy of the typical State excavation permit is 
presented as Figure A-1 in Appendix A. The balance of the excavation 
permits are on file with the Project Office. 
5. 
Coordination of utility toning and clearance of the boring/SCPTu locations 
with various utility companies. 
6. 
Provision of police officers and safety devices for traffic control and/or lane 
closures at selected boring/SCPTu locations during our field exploration 
program. 
7. 
Implementation of best management practices (BMPs) during our field 
exploration work within the highway and in other paved areas.

Mobilization and demobilization of truck-mounted drill rigs, water trucks,

Drilling and sampling of twenty-seven (27) borings extending to depths 
ranging from about 88 to 208 feet below the existing ground/pavement

SECTION 3.0 - SITE CHARACTERIZATION 
3.1 
Regional Geology 
The Island of Oahu generally is composed of the weathered and deeply 
dissected remnants of two extinct shield (domed) volcanoes – Waianae and Koolau. 
The Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes formed during the late Pliocene and early 
Pleistocene Epochs by the sequential deposition of basaltic lava flows. The older 
Waianae Volcano forms the bulk of the western third of Oahu’s landmass while the 
younger Koolau Volcano forms a major portion of the eastern two-thirds of Oahu’s 
landmass.  
Generally, it is believed that the Waianae and Koolau Shield Volcanoes began 
erupting on the order of about 20 to 25 million years ago as deep ocean submarine 
volcanoes. After a long period of eruptive activity, major sub aerial volcanic eruptions on 
the Island of Oahu ceased approximately 2.2 to 3.5 million years ago in the Pleistocene 
Epoch. Furthermore, the Waianae Volcano became extinct while the Koolau Volcano 
remained active; therefore the eroded eastern flank of the Waianae Volcano is partially 
overlapped and buried below the younger Koolau Volcano lavas near the central interior 
portion of the Island of Oahu.  
Following the cessation of the major Koolau volcanic activity, a long period of 
erosion was experienced before some late-stage volcanic activity resumed with the 
eruption of Honolulu Volcanic Series materials approximately 400,000 to 800,000 years 
ago, mainly at the southern and southeastern portions of the Island of Oahu. The late 
stage eruption of the Honolulu Volcanic Series is responsible for some additional 
localized lava flows and the formation of several prominent pyroclastic cinder and tuff 
cones in the Honolulu area and vicinity.  
Finally, land development within the last 100 years along the proposed transit 
corridor has brought the area to its present form. Land use and other development 
projects have resulted in alterations to the natural terrain including the placement of 
earth fills, which may be of poor quality in terms of supporting large structural loads. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 10 
Hawaii ● California 
Evidence from previous deep drilled wells indicates that the Island of Oahu may 
have subsided by as much as 1,200 feet since the cessation of the early volcanic 
activity in the Pleistocene Epoch (Macdonald and Abbott, 1970). During this period of 
slow island subsidence, coral-algal reefs grew and fossilized along the southern coast 
of the Island of Oahu. As a result, broad embayment’s formed that were bounded by 
fringing barrier reefs whose growth essentially kept pace with the slow rate of island 
subsidence. Thereafter, a series of shallow lagoons formed behind the barrier reefs and 
both terrigenous and marine sediments accumulated within the lagoons (Macdonald 
and Abbott, 1970). 
In addition to island subsidence during the Pleistocene Epoch (most recent Ice 
Age), many sea-level fluctuations occurred as a result of the waxing and waning of 
global continental glaciation. Generally speaking, as the great continental glaciers 
accumulated, the global level of the oceans fell because there was less water available 
to fill the oceanic basins. Conversely, as the glaciers receded (melted), the global level 
of the oceans rose because of the water influx. The sea level fluctuations resulted in 
stands of the sea that were higher and lower relative to the present sea-level conditions. 
The following are some generalized examples of the geomorphic effects that resulted 
from the changes in sea level relative to the island landmass. 
The higher sea-level stands caused the accumulation of deltas and depositional 
fans composed of terrigenous sediments at the heads of old bays. Furthermore, the 
higher sea stands caused accumulation of coralline reef materials at correspondingly 
higher elevations and deposition of lagoonal and marine sediments in quiet backwaters 
protected from wave action by fringing coral reefs. 
The lower sea-level stands caused streams to incise erosional valleys into the 
existing sediments and reef platforms. In addition, the sub aerial exposure of the marine 
sediments and calcareous materials caused consolidation of the soft deltaic materials 
and/or lagoonal deposits and hardening of the calcareous reef materials. Finally, 
renewed sub aerial erosion (acting on the upper regions of the volcanic dome) caused 
the mobilization and deposition of terrigenous alluvial soils within relatively high energy 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 11 
Hawaii ● California 
depositional environments, which is typified by a poorly sorted mix of alluvial materials 
with a greater range in particle sizes. 
During periods of no significant sea level change, continued stream flow 
extended the alluvial deltas and fans seaward and allowed the deposition of alluvium 
overlying pre-existing lagoonal sediments. 
The proposed Kamehameha Highway Segment of the transit corridor, extending 
from Pearl City to Aiea, traverses generally east to west across lowland coastal alluvial 
plains along the southern distal flank of the Koolau Mountain Range. The alluvial plain is 
northerly from and borders the East Loch estuary of Pearl Harbor. The East Loch of 
Pearl Harbor resides at the confluence of several well-established natural drainage 
channels emanating from the higher elevations of the Koolau Mountain Range. The 
drainages extend in a generally north to south orientation across the transit corridor 
alignment. The eastern two-thirds of the Kamehameha Highway Segment is generally 
about 1,500 linear feet or less from the existing shoreline bordering the East Loch of 
Pearl Harbor. 
The coastal plain surfaces traversed by the Kamehameha Highway Segment are 
relatively flat to gently undulating in the easterly to westerly direction. The Kamehameha 
Highway Segment crosses five primary stream channels that discharge at the East Loch 
of Pearl Harbor. The meandering streams have contributed significantly to the erosional 
evolution and deposition of the coastal alluvial plains by incising into the underlying 
basaltic rock (Koolau Basalt) comprising the basement rock formation of this portion of 
the Island of Oahu.  
As the sea-level fluctuated in response to world-wide glacial stages, the stream 
channels and surrounding environments were repeatedly drowned and emerged. The 
cyclic drowning and re-emergence of the lowland shoreline environment over time 
created varying geological depositional environments. The changing depositional 
environments in turn affected the types of sedimentary materials that were deposited to 
fill the existing channel incisions and mantle the underlying the basement rock material. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 12 
Hawaii ● California 
It should be noted that two existing pyroclastic tuff cone vents (Aliamanu and 
Makalapa Craters) related to the late Honolulu Volcanic Series eruptions are present 
approximately 1.7 miles toward the southeast from the eastern end of the Kamehameha 
Highway Segment. The koko-head portion of the alignment traverses area known to be 
overlain by Honolulu Volcanics, e.g., the Tuff, as evidenced in Borings B-16, B-17, 
B-435, and B-436 drilled along the far-eastern end of the alignment in Aiea. 
The Kamehameha Highway Segment alignment is located within the Southern 
Oahu regional aquifer system, which is the largest potable basal groundwater area 
within the Island of Oahu. Basalt rock from the Koolau Volcano comprises the primary 
groundwater aquifer in the vicinity of the Kamehameha Highway Segment alignment. 
Recharge of this regional aquifer generally occurs from the higher inland elevations. 
The regional flow of groundwater is generally from inland toward the low elevation 
coastal areas. However, both unconsolidated and consolidated sedimentary deposits 
form a thick low permeability confining geologic unit that is locally termed “caprock” near 
the coast. These sediments overlie the buried basaltic rock and act as a low 
permeability confining layer overlying the more permeable basaltic rock aquifer.  
On the Pearl City/Aiea coastal plains and vicinity, the sedimentary wedge of 
alluvial materials also is referred to as caprock overlying the basal aquifer. The capping 
material keeps the basal aquifer under a confining water pressure; therefore, artesian 
flow conditions may be encountered when the basal aquifer with sufficient groundwater 
head is pierced and a conduit is provided extending toward the ground surface. 
In general, groundwater levels in the Southern Oahu regional aquifer area may 
range from about 25 to 30 feet above sea level inland to about 15 to 20 feet above sea 
level near the shore where the groundwater is under artesian pressure beneath the 
confining caprock material (USGS Publication HA 730-N). 
In general, earthquakes associated with volcanic activity are most common on 
the Island of Hawaii. Earthquakes that are directly associated with the movement of 
magma are concentrated beneath the active Kilauea and Mauna Loa Volcanoes on the 
Island of Hawaii. Because the majority of the earthquakes in Hawaii (over 90 percent) 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 13 
Hawaii ● California 
are related to volcanic activity, the risk of high seismic activity and degree of ground 
shaking diminishes with increased distance from the Island of Hawaii. 
Due to the relatively short period of documented earthquake monitoring in the 
State of Hawaii, information pertaining to earthquakes that were felt on the Island of 
Oahu may not be complete. In general, we are not aware of reported earthquakes 
greater than Magnitude 6 occurring on the Island of Oahu over the last 150 years of 
recorded history. Based on the seismic activity on the Island of Oahu, the specified amax 
for Southern Oahu is 0.18g (on rocky ground conditions) according to the guidelines 
provided by the State of Hawaii - Department of Transportation (HDOT). 
3.2 
Subsurface Characteristics 
In general, we observed five main stratigraphic units within the Kamehameha 
Highway Segment extending from Pearl City to Aiea. The stratigraphic units consist of 
soil and rock materials of varying character and engineering properties. The five main 
stratigraphic units are listed below in general order of geologic age, beginning with the 
oldest unit and ending with the youngest unit: 
Tertiary Koolau Basalt (Tkb) 
Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
Recent Fill (Rf) 
The following sections briefly describe the general characteristics of each of the 
above stratigraphic units based on our literature research, past experience with similar 
materials, and the field exploration and laboratory testing program conducted at 
selected locations along the project segment. The approximate delineation of these 
stratigraphic units at the ground surface along the segment alignment is presented on 
the Geology Map, Plate 4. 
3.2.1 Tertiary Koolau Basalt (Tkb) 
Koolau Basalt is the oldest geologic unit within the segment alignment extending 
from Pearl City to Aiea. Generally, it consists of pahoehoe flows of basaltic lavas 
and may have a mantle of cobbles and boulders, or residual soils, overlying the 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 14 
Hawaii ● California 
top of the basalt rock formation. In addition, it is anticipated that various types of 
volcanic rock sub-components, such as clinker layers and lava tubes, also may 
be encountered within the basalt rock formation. 
Structurally, Koolau Basalt and its subunits are among the strongest of all the 
units encountered within the project alignment under consideration. Therefore, 
the basalt formation and its related residual soils and saprolites make excellent 
load supporting capacity units and/or bearing formations due to their high 
strength characteristics in an undisturbed state. However, its use for foundation 
support in some areas of the project segment is limited by the considerable 
depths required to reach the top of the basalt and by the artesian groundwater 
conditions expected where the basalt is encountered at relatively shallow depths. 
The following subunits of the Koolau Basalt are used in this geotechnical data 
report: 
Residual Soil 
This subunit consists of soils produced from the in-situ chemical 
weathering of the basic rock originating from Koolau Basalt. These soils 
generally consist of stiff to very stiff, brownish red or reddish brown clayey 
silts and silty clays. The soils are usually “structureless” and may have a 
blocky texture. 
Saprolite 
Saprolite is essentially derived from the tropical weathering of basaltic 
formations, which may contain intrusive rock bodies of variable 
composition. Saprolite is a form of residual soil that exhibits a relict 
structure from the parent rock. Structural features, such as vesicles and 
joints, are visible in saprolite. This subunit consists of predominantly 
clayey silt and silty clay soils containing variations in color with varying 
degrees of mottling. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 15 
Hawaii ● California 
Clinker 
Clinker is the fragmental basalt found on the top, and sometimes at the 
bottom, of a’a lava flows. Clinker generally is gravel to cobble-sized 
fragments of scoriaceous rock in a tightly packed but open-graded 
structure. 
Basalt Formation 
This subunit is used to designate the in-situ lava formation. This subunit 
occurs as red, brown, or gray, severely fractured to massive, medium hard 
to very hard rock formation. 
3.2.2 Older Alluvium (Qa) 
Older alluvial deposits are generally very stiff to hard silty and clayey soils that 
originally were deposited in alluvial environments, buried over long periods of 
time by more recent deposits to become somewhat consolidated, and then 
subsequently uncovered at the ground surface by erosion or other geologic 
processes. This unit generally overlies the Koolau Basalt and consists of 
terrigenous sediments, which have been transported by stream action from the 
upper reaches of the streams.  
Gravel lenses representing buried stream channel are sometimes encountered. 
Portions of the Older Alluvium unit contain some weathered basaltic cobbles and 
boulders. These coarse-grained parts of the unit also may contain artesian 
groundwater from the basal fresh water system or as a sub-aquifer. The deposits 
may resemble saprolite soil deposits, but they are differentiable by the layered 
characteristics. 
Older Alluvium materials generally exhibit high strength, which leads to a 
relatively high bearing or load carrying capacity with generally low settlement 
characteristics. Frequently, these materials show a moderate to high shrink/swell 
potential when subjected to fluctuations in moisture content. However, this is not 
considered a constraint for the type of structural systems proposed for this 
project. Structurally, the Older Alluvium materials are considered to be sufficiently 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 16 
Hawaii ● California 
competent to support relatively large structural loads with low settlement 
characteristics when designed properly. 
3.2.3 Quaternary Honolulu Tuffs (Qht) 
Tuff from vents of the Honolulu Volcanic Series consists of volcanic ash, which 
consolidated or hardened to form a medium hard rock that resembles siltstone or 
sandstone, locally called “mudrock.” The tuff is frequently found overlying 
coralline deposits or alluvium units. It should be noted that it is quite common to 
see a coarsening from accidental detritus and/or agglomeration toward the 
bottoms of the ash beds.  
Because the formation is a medium hard rock, it has very high strength 
characteristics. Structurally, the tuff is very competent. This formation has 
relatively low permeability, and groundwater generally is not encountered in this 
formation. When it does occur, it is usually in the form of seepage along joints. 
3.2.4 Recent Alluvium (Ra) 
These soils are generally the result of sedimentation in a very low energy 
environment such as an estuary or bay. Frequently, the soils assigned to this unit 
accumulated very rapidly under water and might not have been exposed to air. 
Therefore, there is a tendency to encounter under-consolidated sediments, which 
settle under the weight of minimal overburden or structural loading. Recent 
alluvium generally is found within some stream channels and bordering some 
stream banks, at the mouths (outlets) of streams, and within broad valley floors 
and basin features surrounded by hills. These soils also may be found in 
proximity to swamp and tidal flat environments. 
The soils assigned to this unit generally are considered poorly to very poorly 
suited for use as support for foundation loads, due to their low in-situ strength 
characteristics and the tendency for excessive settlement to occur in structures 
overlying soils of this unit. The following subunits are used in this geotechnical 
data report. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 17 
Hawaii ● California 
Recent Alluvium 
This subunit generally consists of tan to brown silty clays with varying 
amounts of sands and infrequent pockets of gravel and cobbles. The 
consistency is generally in the range of very soft to medium stiff. 
Lagoonal Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of light gray to medium gray sandy silts or 
silty sands in a very soft to soft consistency and/or very loose to loose 
relative density. Gravel sized fragments are rare. Frequently, the silts are 
organic in origin and may have a mild to strong organic odor. 
Marsh Deposit 
This subunit generally consists of dark gray and brown silts and clays with 
fibrous organic materials in a very soft to soft consistency. This soil unit 
contains traces of fine to medium grained sands. They are characterized 
by a moderate to strong organic odor and may contain small fragile 
mollusk shells. 
3.2.5 Recent Fill (Rf) 
This stratigraphic unit represents low lying areas that were filled during 
urbanization. The majority of these low lying areas is underlain at relatively 
shallow depths by recent alluvium and was originally marsh areas. A small part of 
the filled lands along the segment alignment are underlain by older alluvial soil 
deposits. In areas underlain by the recent alluvium, the fill materials generally 
consist of clayey silts and sandy silts with stiff to hard consistencies. The fills 
were intended only to support very light structures on shallow spread footings; 
therefore, the fills only form a thin crust over the soft alluvial soils. 
The quality and composition of the fill materials are variable. These fills were 
frequently placed without proper compaction control. Therefore, it is difficult to 
make any general statements regarding their engineering properties or 
construction in these areas. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 3 – SITE CHARACTERIZATION  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 18 
Hawaii ● California 
3.2.6 Groundwater 
We encountered groundwater in the borings drilled along the project alignment at 
depths ranging from about 4 to 41 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface 
at the time of our field exploration. The groundwater levels measured generally 
correspond to about Elevations -19 to +22 feet Mean Sea Level (MSL). Experience 
has shown that groundwater levels are seasonally dependent.  
In addition, we wish to point out that we encountered artesian groundwater 
conditions in Boring Nos. 117 and 119 (B-117 and B-119) at various depths below 
the existing ground surface during the course of our drilling operations. 
Groundwater levels were measured during advancement of the boreholes drilled 
for the project at the times shown on the Logs of Borings presented in Appendix A 
and summarized in Table F-1 of Appendix F.

SECTION 4.0 - GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM 
As noted previously, the geotechnical program was scoped based on existing 
information circa late 2008 with primary emphasis on suitable foundations for the aerial 
guideway. An effort was made to co-locate investigations with appurtenant structures to 
the extent practicable. 
The geotechnical exploration program conducted for the Kamehameha Highway 
Segment of the HHCTCP generally consisted of the following field and laboratory work 
components. A brief description of each work component is presented below. Detailed 
discussions, findings, and test results of the various components are provided in the 
respective appendices herein. 
In addition, subsurface information along the Kamehameha Highway Segment 
also are available from the geotechnical engineering exploration and geological 
reconnaissance conducted in 1990 and 1991 for the Rapid Transit System project. 
4.1 
Soil Borings, Rock Coring, and Associated Field Testing 
Soil and rock sampling methods and field testing are described in more detail in 
the introduction to Appendix A. The following discussion provides an overview of the 
methods and procedures. 
Typically, the soil borings were advanced with a combination of 4-inch diameter 
continuous-flight solid-stem augers and continued with PQ-size wireline coring methods 
to advance the PQ size casing. Sampling of the soils was performed using the modified 
California sampler (ASTM D 3550) and the Standard Penetration Test (SPT) split spoon 
sampler (ASTM D 1586). In the softer soils, relatively “undisturbed” and “undisturbed” 
samples were obtained for testing by driving a ring-lined modified California Split 
Sampler (ASTM D 3550) and pushing a 3-inch OD thin-walled stainless steel Shelby 
tube (ASTM D 1587) into the soil formation.  
Where the borehole was advanced utilizing the PQ core barrel in the soil 
formations, samples of the soils recovered from the PQ core barrel also were obtained 
and retained in core boxes for visual observation. In some cases, the borehole was 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 4 – GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 20 
Hawaii ● California 
advanced utilizing the PQ casing only as a rotary wash drilling method; therefore, there 
were no soil samples retrieved between the soil sampling points (soil sampling by the 
modified California sampler or the split spoon sampler for the Standard Penetration 
Test). The borings were advanced into the rock formations with the PQ-size wireline 
coring methods. The PQ-size wireline coring methods produce rock core samples that 
are 3.35 inches (or 85 mm) in diameter. 
In-situ testing of the subsurface materials in the borings included the SPT, which 
was performed in accordance with ASTM D 1586. SPT sampling provides a measure of 
the resistance or consistency of a soil and is reported as the uncorrected N-value, which 
is defined as the number of blows required to drive a standard split-spoon sampler 
12 inches with a 140-lb hammer, after 6 inches of initial seating. The penetration 
resistance of the modified California sampler was recorded in a similar manner, and the 
modified California sampler blows per 12 inches can be approximately related to the 
SPT N-value through correlation. 
Rock core recovery (REC) and rock quality designation (RQD) were measured 
as described in Appendix A. ASTM D 6032 - Standard Test Method for Determining 
Rock Quality Designation (RQD) of Rock Core states that “Core sizes from BQ to PQ 
with core diameters of 36.5 mm (1.44 in.) to 85 mm (3.35 in.), respectively, are normally 
acceptable for measuring RQD as long as proper drilling techniques are used that do 
not cause excessive core breakage or poor recovery, or both. The NX-size [54.7 mm 
(2.16 in.)] and NQ-size [47.5 mm (1.87 in.)] are the optimal core sizes for measuring 
RQD. The RQD is also useful for large core diameters provided the core diameter is 
clearly stated.” The rock core photographs are included in Appendix H for information 
and reference. 
During the field exploration program, our geologists performed pocket 
penetrometer tests on selected cohesive soil samples and point load tests on selected 
rock specimens. Pocket penetrometer tests provide an indication of the unconfined 
compressive strength of a soil sample. Point load tests are index strength tests for rock 
materials. The pocket penetrometer test values are shown on the Logs of Borings in 
Appendix A, and the point load test results are included in Appendix E. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 4 – GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 21 
Hawaii ● California 
4.2 
Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing 
Seismic piezocone penetration testing (SCPTu) was performed at four selected 
locations, using seismic piezocone equipment, also referred to as Seismic CPT 
(SCPTu). The test procedures are discussed in more detail in Appendix B. In brief, a 
steel cone connected to a friction sleeve is pushed hydraulically into the soil, and the tip 
resistance, sleeve friction, and piezometric pore pressures are measured, providing 
information that can be correlated to the soil type and behavior in a virtually continuous 
profile with depth. SCPTu testing can be performed only to the depths to which the cone 
tip can be hydraulically pushed with our truck-mounted drill rig set up. At the 
three locations tested, all of which were in areas of recent alluvium, the SCPTu was 
terminated at depths of about 34 to 71 feet below existing grade. 
The seismic cone tip contains a geophone sensor. At a succession of depths, the 
advancement of the cone tip was halted, a seismic pulse was generated at the ground 
surface by striking a loaded plank with a switched hammer, and the resulting seismic 
shear wave was detected at the geophone. The distance from the source and the wave 
arrival time allow for determination of the average shear wave velocity over a depth 
interval. The seismic piezocone penetration test records are presented in Appendix B. 
4.3 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at three (3) of the 
completed boreholes, using seismic piezocone penetration testing (SCPTu) equipment. 
Procedures are discussed in more detail in Appendix C. In brief, the completed borehole 
was tremie-filled with coated bentonite pellets in order to provide a medium through 
which the seismic cone tip could be pushed. The sleeve and tip resistance through the 
bentonite-filled borehole are not meaningful because the cone is pushed through the 
bentonite backfill; however, the seismic wave travels from the ground surface through 
the surrounding soils, allowing measurement of the average seismic shear wave 
velocity in the same manner as described above for the SCPTu. The measured average 
seismic shear wave velocity logs are presented in Appendix C. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SECTION 4 – GEOTECHNICAL EXPLORATION PROGRAM  
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
Page 22 
Hawaii ● California 
4.4 
Geophysical Investigations 
Surface geophysical seismic refraction surveys using the Microtremor Array 
(SeisOptReMi) methods were performed by Zonge Geosciences, Inc. along nine (9) 
profile lines. The profile lines were focused primarily in areas of recent alluvium where 
the objective was to obtain estimates of the depth and lateral extent of soft soil areas. 
The methodology and results of the geophysical investigations are presented in 
Appendix D. 
4.5 
Piezometric Water Level Monitoring 
Vibrating wire piezometers were installed at three test boring locations to monitor 
piezometric water levels and their seasonal fluctuations. However, the cables for three 
piezometers were severed during installation in one of the borings (B-120). 
Two piezometer tips were installed at varying depths at each of the remaining two 
boring locations to measure groundwater gradients. Data was recorded with a 
datalogger from the time of installation (in June and September 2008) through the 
termination of reporting in February 2010. Details of the piezometer installation and 
piezometric level readings are included in Appendix F. 
4.6 
Laboratory Testing 
Laboratory testing was performed on soil and rock samples to determine the 
physical and engineering properties. Laboratory tests performed included moisture 
content, unit weight, Atterberg limits, grain size distribution, unconfined compressive 
strength of soil and rock, unconsolidated-undrained (UU) triaxial compression, 
consolidated-undrained (CU) triaxial compression, direct shear, consolidation, corrosion 
potential tests (pH, minimum resistivity, chloride content, and sulfate content), and 
organic content. The laboratory test results are provided in Appendix G. 
4.7 
Additional Subsurface Information 
Selected boring logs and laboratory test data from the 1990 and 1991 studies for 
the Rapid Transit System project that are applicable to the Kamehameha Highway 
Segment are included in Appendix I for information and reference purposes.

SECTION 5.0 - LIMITATIONS 
The geotechnical field and laboratory test data submitted in this geotechnical 
data report are based upon information obtained from the field exploration points. 
Variations of subsurface conditions between and beyond the exploration points may 
occur, and the nature and extent of these variations may not become evident until 
additional field investigation and/or construction is underway. 
The locations and elevations of the field exploration points (with the following 
exceptions) indicated in this report have been surveyed by ControlPoint Surveying, Inc. 
following the completion of our field exploration program. The location and elevation of 
B-226 was surveyed by R.M. Towill Corporation following the completion of our field 
exploration program. Northing and easting coordinates for B-434 were converted from 
the latitude and longitude coordinates obtained from our handheld GPS unit while the 
boring elevation is based on interpolation between contours shown on the Topographic 
Survey Map provided by PB Americas, Inc. 
The stratification lines shown on the graphic representations of the field 
exploration points depict the approximate boundaries between soil and rock types and, 
as such, may denote a gradual transition. Water level data presented on the logs of 
borings in this report were based on measurements recorded in the borings at the times 
shown on the graphic representations. It should be noted that fluctuation of the water 
level may occur due to variation in tides, rainfall, temperature, and other factors. 
This report has been prepared for the exclusive use of PB Americas, Inc. and 
their client, City and County of Honolulu, Department of Transportation Services, for 
specific application to the Kamehameha Highway Segment (Pearl City to Aiea) of the 
Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project in accordance with generally accepted 
geotechnical engineering principles and practices. No warranty is expressed or implied. 
This geotechnical data report presents information on the general subsurface 
conditions for a follow-on design-level geotechnical investigation. This geotechnical data 
report is limited in scope and is for the project design advancement and cost estimation 
purposes only. The Project Geotechnical Engineer should conduct a detailed, 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010

site-specific geotechnical investigation based on the actual project structure locations

The borings for this study did not encounter underground structures, such as

In the event that these items are encountered during construction, the Project 
Geotechnical Engineer should provide appropriate recommendations for treatment

This geotechnical data report was not intended to investigate the potential for

A P P E N D I X   A 
Test Boring Exploration 
We explored the subsurface conditions along the Kamehameha Highway Segment 
extending from Pearl City to Aiea of the HHCTCP by drilling and sampling 27 borings, 
designated as Boring Nos. B-114 through B-120, B-226 through B-238, B-434 through 
B-436, and B-501 through B-504. The borings extended to depths ranging from about 
88 to 208 feet below the existing ground/pavement surface using various truck-mounted 
drill rigs equipped with continuous-flight augers and rotary coring tools. The field 
exploration was conducted from June 4, 2008 through December 10, 2009. The boring 
locations are summarized in Table A-1 and are shown on the Boring Location Plans, 
Plates 3.1 through 3.8. A copy of the typical excavation permits from the State of Hawaii – 
Department of Transportation, Highways Division is presented as Figure A-1 in Appendix 
A. The balance of the excavation permits are on file with the Project Office. 
Soil borings were advanced with a combination of 4-inch ID continuous-flight 
solid-stem augers and continued with PQ-size wireline coring methods to advance PQ size 
casing. After the borings were advanced to the maximum depth of the boreholes, the 
bored holes (with the exception of those being converted to groundwater monitoring 
points) generally were backfilled with 0.25-inch diameter coated bentonite pellets (below 
the groundwater level) and bentonite chips (above the groundwater level). The temporary 
casing from the coring operations was used as a tremie pipe to place the bentonite pellets 
starting from the bottom and advancing upward.  Backfilling above the static groundwater 
table were performed in stages by adding water to limited amount of bentonite chips to 
ensure hydration before additional bentonite chips were placed in the borehole. The top 
3 feet of the borehole was then grouted with Quikcrete concrete mix. No additives were 
added to the drilling water during our drilling operations. 
Our geologists/engineers classified the materials encountered in the borings by 
visual and textural examination in the field in general accordance with ASTM D 2488, 
Standard Practice for Description and Identification of Soils, and monitored the drilling 
operations on a near-continuous basis. These classifications were further reviewed 
visually and by testing in the laboratory. Soils were classified in general accordance with 
ASTM D 2487, Standard Practice for Classification of Soils for Engineering Purposes 
(Unified Soil Classification System), as shown on the Soil Log Legend, Plate A-0.1. 
Graphic representations of the materials encountered and other observations, such as 
groundwater levels, artesian groundwater condition, drilling rate, etc. are presented on the 
Logs of Borings, Plates A-1 through A-28. 
Relatively “undisturbed” soil samples were obtained from the drilled borings in 
general accordance with ASTM D 3550, Standard Practice for Thick Wall, Ring-Lined, 
Spilt Barrel, Drive Sampling of Soils, by driving a 3-inch OD modified California sampler 
with a 140-pound hammer falling 30 inches. In addition, some samples were obtained from 
the drilled borings in general accordance with ASTM D 1586, Standard Test Method for 
Penetration Test and Split-Barrel Sampling of Soils, by driving a 2-inch OD standard 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Appendix A 
Test Boring Exploration 
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2010     Page A-2 
Hawaii ● California 
penetration sampler using the same hammer and drop. The blow counts needed to drive 
the sampler the second and third 6 inches of an 18-inch drive are shown as the 
“Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. 
“Undisturbed” samples of the soft soil formation were obtained by pushing a 3-inch OD 
thin-walled stainless steel Shelby tube into the soil formation in general accordance with 
ASTM D 1587, Standard Practice for Thin-Walled Tube Sampling of Soils for Geotechnical 
Purposes. The range of hydraulic pressures needed to advance the sampler through a 
24-inch push is shown as the “Penetration Resistance” on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths. 
Pocket penetrometer tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples 
retrieved in the field. The pocket penetrometer test provides an indication of the 
unconfined compressive strength of the soil sample. Pocket penetrometer test results are 
summarized on Tables G-1.1 through G-1.17 and are presented on the Logs of Borings at 
the appropriate sample depths. 
Field torvane shear tests were performed on selected cohesive soil samples 
retrieved in the field. The field torvane shear tests provide an indication of the undrained 
shear strength of the soil sample. Results of the field torvane shear tests are presented 
on the Logs of Borings at the appropriate sample depths. The test results have already 
taken into account of the different vane blade sizes used in the testing. 
Core samples of the rock materials encountered at the project site were obtained 
by using diamond core drilling techniques, consisting of a PQ wireline coring system 
with double tube core barrel (core size of 3.35 inches), in general accordance with 
ASTM D 2113, Diamond Core Drilling for Site Investigation. Core drilling is a rotary 
drilling method that uses a hollow bit to cut into the rock formation. The rock material left 
in the hollow core of the bit is mechanically recovered for examination and description. 
The coring rates indicated on the Logs of Borings refer to the time required to complete 
that particular core run at that depth. Rock cores were described in general accordance 
with the Rock Description System, as shown on the Rock Log Legend, Plate A-0.2. The 
Rock Description System is based on the publication “Suggested Methods for the 
Quantitative Description of Discontinuities in Rock Masses” by International Society for 
Rock Mechanics (March 1977). 
Recovery (REC) may be used as a subjective guide to the interpretation of the 
relative quality of rock masses, where appropriate. Recovery is defined as the actual 
length of material recovered from a coring attempt versus the length of the core attempt. 
For example, if 3.7 feet of material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run, the recovery 
would be 74 percent and would be shown on the Logs of Borings as REC = 74%. 
The Rock Quality Designation (RQD) is also a subjective guide to the relative 
quality of rock masses. RQD is defined as the percentage of the core run in rock that is 
sound rock material in excess of 4 inches in length without any discontinuities, discounting 
any drilling, mechanical, and handling induced fractures or breaks. If 2.5 feet of sound 
material is recovered from a 5.0-foot core run in rock, the RQD would be 50 percent and 
would be shown on the Logs of Borings as RQD = 50%. Generally, the following is used to

The following terms describe the resistance of a rock to indentation or scratching:
Specimen breaks with difficulty after several "pinging" hammer blows.
Example: Dense, fine grain volcanic rock
Specimen breaks with some difficulty after several hammer blows.
Example: Vesicular, vugular, coarse-grained rock
Specimen can be broked by one hammer blow. Cannot be scraped by knife. SPT may penetrate by
~25 blows per inch with bounce.
Example: Porous rock such as clinker, cinder, and coral reef
Can be indented by one hammer blow. Can be scraped or peeled by knife. SPT can penetrate by
~100 blows per foot.
Example: Weathered rock, chalk-like coral reef
Crumbles under hammer blow. Can be peeled and carved by knife. Can be indented by finger
pressure.
Example: Saprolite

A P P E N D I X   B 
Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing 
Geolabs, Inc. conducted cone penetration testing using the seismic piezocone 
penetration testing equipment at four probe locations, designated as B-116(CPT), 
B-119(CPT), B-120(CPT), and B-500(CPT), as shown in the following table and on the 
Boring Location Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.8. 
SEISMIC PIEZOCONE TESTING LOCATIONS 
Boring No. 
Station No. 
Northing 
Easting 
Surface 
Elevation 
(feet MSL) 
B-116(CPT) 
862+30 
79893.941 
1656349.605 
+12.1 
B-119(CPT) 
912+70 
77695.527 
1660880.589 
+12.2 
B-120(CPT) 
934+60 
76496.267 
1662694.175 
+11.9 
B-500(CPT) 
901+20 
78178.545 
1659849.382 
+10.3 
The tests were performed in general accordance with ASTM D 5778 – Standard 
Test Method for Performing Electronic Friction Cone and Piezocone Penetration Testing 
of Soils. The probes extended to depths ranging from about 34 to 71 feet below the 
existing pavement surface using seismic piezocone penetration testing equipment 
(SCPTU) before refusal conditions to our system were encountered. 
The seismic piezocone penetration test consists of hydraulically pushing a 10-ton 
steel electronic subtraction cone (Serial Number DSG1063) manufactured by Vertek 
with an apex angle of 60 degrees and an area of 1.55 square inches (10 square 
centimeters) connected to a friction sleeve with an area of 23.25 square inches 
(150 square centimeters), into the ground at a rate of approximately 20 mm/second 
(+5 mm/second) using a Mobile B-90 truck-mounted drill rig. A catalog cut of the 10-ton 
electronic subtraction cone and the manufacturer calibration report are presented in 
Figures B-1 and B-2 for illustration/information purposes only. 
The tip and side friction resistance and pore pressure induced immediately 
behind the cone tip (designated as the U2 parameter) during penetration are measured 
by strain gauges within the instrument and are recorded versus the depth of penetration 
at about 2-inch (5-centimeter) intervals. When the material encountered was too hard for 
further penetration by the SCPTU equipment, which might cause potential equipment 
damage, penetration was halted and the cone was withdrawn. 
The cone carries a uniaxial horizontal geophone with an embedded inclinometer 
to detect the arrival of a shear wave propagated from the ground surface. The seismic 
measurements are made when the SCPTU has stopped and a shear wave is sent into the 
subsurface. A shear wave is generated at the surface by striking a loaded plank in 
opposite directions with a switched hammer at approximately 6-foot intervals. The 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Appendix B 
Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing 
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2010     Page B-2 
Hawaii ● California 
propagation time of the wave from the hammer blow to the cone was measured at each 
depth interval. The vector difference of these depths divided by the time difference 
provided the average shear wave velocity over the depth interval. 
The field data collected were subsequently processed in the office using the 
computer software program – CPT Coneplot developed by Vertek. Logs of the seismic 
piezocone penetration tests are presented on Plates B-1.1, B-2.1, B-3.1, and B-4.1. In 
general, the plots include cone tip resistance, friction ratio (sleeve friction over tip 
resistance), pore pressure, local friction, shear wave velocity, soil behavior (based on the 
Rf zone), and interpreted soil description. Values of the tip resistance, friction ratio, and 
pore pressure may be correlated to soil strength and classification. The center of the 
friction sleeve is approximately 4 inches (10 cm) behind the cone tip. To calculate the 
friction ratio, the average friction resistance and tip resistance are compared at the same 
depth. This involves an offset of the friction resistance by the physical distance of 
4 inches. The soil behavior is determined using the average tip resistance and the friction 
ratio over approximately 14 inches (35 centimeters) using Robertson’s 1986 soil 
classification chart as shown on Figure B-3. 
Pore pressure is presented in terms of equivalent height of water head (fresh 
water). The equilibrium water pressure was estimated based on depth below water 
surface at the test location and the density of fresh water.  
The shear wave velocities measured at the probe locations are presented on 
Plates B-1.2, B-2.2, B-3.2, and B-4.2. Digital records of the seismic piezocone 
penetration tests are on file in the Project Office.  
In addition, we attempted to correlate the corrected cone tip resistance to the 
estimated undrained shear strengths of the soft soils from the available CPT data.  In 
general, we averaged the cone tip resistance values for every foot of the soil profile 
collected to reduce the amount of scatter in the data.  We then correlated the average 
tip resistance to estimate the undrained shear strengths based on the following equation 
by Robertson, P.K. Campanella, R.G., et. al. (1986).  
Su = (qT – vo) / NKT   
Where 
Su = undrained shear strength 
qT = corrected cone tip resistance 
vo = total overburden stress 
NKT = factor of 18 based on local experience 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Vertek - Hogentogler Cone Penetrometer Products
Please Contact Us at (802) 728-4588 or visit our website at www.vertek.ara.com
Cones & Data Acquisition Systems
10 & 15 Ton Digital Cones for E4FCS
Temperature Compensated - The digital electronic subtraction 10 and 15 
ton cones have been developed to address the accuracy, sensitivity and dura-
bility problems inherent in other cone designs, with a resolution of 6.5 and 10 
Ibs. respectively. The unit consists of a single element strain gauge transducer, 
eliminating mechanical coupling effects, and with cone support electron-
ics packaged directly behind the transducer. Both the cone tip, local friction 
element and pore pressure element are ﬁ eld replaceable by the operator in 
approximately ﬁ ve minutes. The cones are conﬁ gured with one through ﬁ ve 
channel capacity with six channels from which to choose.
Cone Channels:
•  Tip
•  Local Friction
•  Pore Pressure
•  Temperature
•  Inclination
•  Seismic
•  Resistivity Module can be added
There are two possible locations for pore pressure: on the tip or directly be-
hind the tip.
The electronics have precision power supplies for the strain gauges, which 
eliminate the effects of cable resistance on the measurements. Subtraction is 
performed digitally by the CPU and calibration data for tip, local friction and 
pore pressure channels is stored in CPU non-volatile memory.  Digital cone 
calibration is capable of correcting for non-linearity and hysterisis in a way 
that analog circuitry cannot. Digital cones conform to all international stan-
dards.

Cones & Data Acquisition Systems
Electrical Speciﬁ cations
- Power: +12 Volt at 200 mA (max)
- Connector: 10 pin LEMO waterproof connector
- Operating temperature range: 0oC to 60oC
- Storage temperature: -40oC to 70oC
- 4 channel, 24 bit A/D converter for measurement 
   of tip, friction, and pore pressure.  Minimum 
   noise free resolution of 1 part in 65000
- 10 channel, 12 bit A/D converter for measure
   ment of inclination, seismic, temperature and 
   spare channels
- 8 bit microcontroller with 8kb ﬂ ash memory for 
   cone identiﬁ cation and calibration data storage
- 32kb non-volatile ram for data storage
- RS-485 serial communication.  Cable lengths of 
  1000 ft possible.  CRC error checking to prevent 
  transmission of incorrect data
- Cones are interchangeable in the ﬁ eld.  Cones 
   can be calibrated in the ﬁ eld.  All calibration data 
   is contained in the cone
- Compatible with vision cone and fuel ﬂ uorescence  
   detector
Mechanical Speciﬁ cations  (10 Ton / 15 Ton)
Tip
- Tip angle: 60 degree
- Tip area: 10 cm2  / 15 cm2
- Net area ratio: 0.8
- Tip range: 100 Mpa (1044 TSF) / 150 Mpa (1566 
  TSF)
- Tip mechanical overload capacity: 300 %
- Accuracy: 0.2 %
- Temperature compensated
Sleeve
- Sleeve area: 225 cm2
- Sleeve capacity: 1 MPa
- Sleeve mechanical overload capacity: 200 %
- Accuracy:  0.2 %
- Temperature compensated
- Equal end area eliminates pore pressure 
   effects
Pore Pressure Transducer
- Pore pressure range: 3500 kPa (500 psi)
- Pore pressure mechanical overload pres
   sure: greater than 200 % 
- Accuracy: 0.5 %
- Temperature compensated
- Special cones available with three pressure 
   transducers for simultaneous measurement 
   of U1, U2, U3
Inclinometer
- Two channel inclinometer.  Pitch and roll 
   measurement provides information on cone 
   migration
- Uses accelerometers to measure inclina-
   tion.  No moving parts
- Range: 0 – 15 degrees
- Resolution: 0.1 degree
Seismic
- Uni or Biaxial geophone
- Capacity: +/- 2g
- True DC response
- Electronic offset eliminates effect of gravity.
- Digitized in cone and transmitted over se-
   rial port  No extra wires or analog mea-
   surement required

A P P E N D I X   C 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
Seismic shear wave velocity profiling of the subsurface materials was performed 
using Seismic Piezocone Penetration Testing (SCPTu) equipment. Shear wave velocity 
testing using seismic piezocone penetration test equipment was performed at 
three selected boring locations, designated as B-114(SW), B-116(SW), and B-228(SW), 
as shown in the following table and also on the Boring Location Plans, Plates 3.1 
through 3.8. 
SEISMIC SHEAR WAVE VELOCITY TESTING LOCATIONS

+50.7 
The seismic shear wave velocity profiling was performed at approximately 6-foot 
intervals extending to depths ranging from about 34 to 121 feet below the existing 
ground/pavement surface. In order to conduct the shear wave velocity test in the 
borings, the test borings generally were advanced utilizing rotary coring methods to the 
maximum depth of the borings. Logs of the materials encountered in the borings are 
presented in the Logs of Borings in Appendix A.  
After the borings were advanced to the maximum depth of the boreholes, the 
bored holes were backfilled with 0.25-inch diameter coated bentonite pellets. The 
temporary casing from the coring operations was used as a tremie pipe to place the 
bentonite pellets starting from the bottom and advancing upward. When the bentonite 
pellets were in contact with the groundwater in the borehole, the pellets started to 
hydrate slowly. As the bentonite pellets hydrated, they swelled and softened.  
Backfilling above the static groundwater table were performed in stages by 
adding water to limited amount of bentonite pellets to ensure hydration before additional 
bentonite pellets were placed in the borehole. The probes were then pushed through 
the softened bentonite extending to depths ranging from about 34 to 121 feet below the 
existing ground/pavement surface using the seismic piezocone penetration testing 
equipment (SCPTU). It should be noted that the probe could not advance beyond a 
depth of 34 feet in B-114(SW), possibly due to some unknown obstruction or 
misalignment of the drilled hole and the probing operation.  
The seismic shear wave velocity test consists of hydraulically pushing a 10-ton 
steel electronic subtraction cone into the bentonite backfilled borehole. The cone has an 
apex angle of 60 degrees and a projected surface area of 1.55 square inches 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Appendix C 
Seismic Shear Wave Velocity Testing 
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2010     Page C-2 
Hawaii ● California 
(10 square centimeters) equipped with an electronic depth sensor attached to the cone 
rod. The seismic cone carries a uniaxial horizontal geophone to detect the arrival of a 
shear wave generated and propagated from the ground surface. Seismic measurements 
are made when the SCPT has stopped and a shear wave is sent into the subsurface. A 
shear wave is generated at the surface by striking a loaded plank with a switched 
hammer. The propagation time of the wave from the hammer blow to the cone was 
measured at each discrete depth interval. The vector difference of these depths divided 
by the time difference for the shear wave to arrive at the various depths provided the 
average shear wave velocity over the depth interval. 
The shear wave velocities measured and the average shear wave velocities 
calculated for the top 100 feet of the soil profile (where available) at the boring locations 
are presented on Plates C-1.1 through C-3.5. The average shear wave velocities for the 
upper 100 feet of the soil profile were calculated based on the Steps for Site 
Classification procedures described in Table C3.10.3.1-1 of the AASHTO LRFD Bridge 
Design Specifications (Interim 2008).

A P P E N D I X   D 
Refraction MicroTremor Survey 
Surface geophysical seismic refraction surveys using the MicroTremor (ReMi) 
method were performed at two stages of the project by Zonge Geosciences, Inc. at 
nine (9) selected locations, designated as ReMi Lines 6 to 14, along the segment under 
consideration in this geotechnical data report. The survey results are used to provide an 
estimate on the depth to competent materials (basalt formation) and to estimate the 
depths of the soft soil deposits. In addition, the survey also provides an estimate of the 
seismic shear wave velocity of the subsurface materials. 
Detailed discussions and findings of the 2008 (Lines 6 through 12) and 2009 
(Lines 13 and 14) ReMi surveys for Line 6 through Line 14 are presented in the report 
entitled “Geophysical Investigation Report, 2D Seismic Refraction MicroTremor Survey, 
Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project, Pearl City to Halawa Stream, Ewa, 
Oahu, Hawaii“ dated January 2010 prepared by Zonge Geosciences, Inc. A copy of the 
report is included in this appendix for reference. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
GEOLABS, INC. 
Hawaii ● California 
Page Intentionally Left Blank for Double-Sided Printing 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
ZONGE GEOSCIENCES, INC. 
GEOPHYSICAL INVESTIGATION REPORT 
2D Seismic Refraction MicroTremor Survey 
Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project 
Pearl City to Halawa Stream 
Ewa, Oahu, Hawaii 
Prepared for: 
Robin M. Lim, P.E. 
Geolabs, Inc. 
2006 Kalihi Street 
Honolulu, Hawaii 96819 
February 2010 
Zonge Project #08126/09138 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
2 
Executive Summary 
Two-dimensional (2D) seismic refraction microtremor (ReMi) data were acquired, 
processed and interpreted for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project 
– Pearl City to Halawa Stream - Ewa, Oahu, Hawaii.  The primary objective was to 
determine the lateral and vertical extent of soft-soil conditions; and, a secondary 
objective was to determine the ability of passive-wave seismic surveying to image 
the depth-to-bedrock.  Approximately 1.47-line miles (7,760 line feet) of 2D ReMi 
data were acquired along nine lines.  Line numbers start at Line 6 as this is a 
continuation of an ongoing project.  All nine lines were positioned along the 
Farrington Highway. Seismic line locations were selected based on known and 
suspect soft soil conditions based on geotechnical drilling.  Results indicate the 
seismic and geologic/geotechnical data could be integrated to yield useful 
information beneath the area investigated.  The seismic survey results provide good 
information regarding the presence of- and extent of- soft soils (soft soils are 
defined by the IBC Seismic Design Code).  Additionally, the 2D seismic survey 
revealed the relief on the basaltic bedrock is considerable; it can be encountered as 
shallow as ~45 feet to as deep as ~230 feet beneath the existing highway.  Quality 
of the seismic data was very good, and a high degree of confidence is placed on the 
results presented herein.   
Introduction  
In August 2008, and October 2009 Zonge Geosciences, Inc. (Zonge) conducted a 
seismic surveys for the Honolulu High-Capacity Transit Corridor Project 
(HHCTCP) under subcontract to Geolabs, Inc. (Geolabs), who is a geotechnical 
sub-consultant to PB Americas on the project.  Figure 1 outlines the general project 
area, which covers from Pearl City on the west end to Aiea on the east end.  The 
seismic investigation was performed under the direction of Robin Lim, Vice 
President of Geolabs, as part of the on-going geotechnical investigation.  Geolabs 
laid out the seismic line positions and lengths based on design needs and in 
conjunction with the PB Americas project team. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
3 
Objectives of the seismic investigation were: 
1) Determine the lateral variability of the ‘soft’ soils deposits; and, 
2) Determine depth-to-bedrock. 
To meet these two objectives Zonge completed a two-dimensional (2D) refraction 
microtremor (ReMi) survey with intensive support from Geolabs. Geolabs support 
included traffic control, police escort, and daily logistical input from Mr. Rick Pu of 
Geolabs.  Site conditions along the HHTCP were very similar throughout the 
geophysical survey area. Seismic data were collected along the Farrington Highway, 
US90 (Figure 1) where seismic lines were placed in traffic lanes (east or west 
bound), the median, or on a sidewalk depending on crew safety, day of the week, 
and accessibility.  The line layout portion of this survey required day-to-day 
attention by Geolabs personnel and Zonge is extremely appreciative of the support 
provided during the field investigation.  Additionally, Geolabs provided follow-up 
data, in the form of geologic boring logs and shear-wave velocity data from CPT 
logs that assisted with the interpretation.  As will be discussed in the results section 
of this report, Zonge was provided project stationing in the form of Track 
Alignment Plan and Profile drawings from Geolabs.  We understand that the 
stationing has changed occasionally, so the data (stationing and elevation) presented 
herein have been plotted according to the June 16, 2009 plan and profile drawings 
and the topographic map (titled SJ-topo480.dwg) sent in November, 2009. 
The geologic setting for this project varies considerably along the survey area, but 
generally there are two layers – undifferentiated overburden soils overlying 
bedrock.  However, boring logs indicate the overlying soils are composed of a 
complex series of coarse-grained loose to dense sands and gravels (with cobbles) 
and with a range of fine-grained soft to very stiff clay.  In addition to this general 
soil sequence, interbedded layers of coralline, mudflow or clinker deposits were 
encountered within the overburden soils.  Bedrock is defined as basalt across the 
entire length of the seismic survey area; although, the basaltic bedrock has 
significant variability in the degree of weathering and/or fracture density.  Based on 
years of boring and geotechnical data collection in this area, the depth to bedrock 
was anticipated to vary considerably across the project site, with no consistent trend 
(i.e., a regional dip).  Topographic relief on the bedrock surface is primarily 
attributed to erosional features such as paleo-channels or relief caused at the time 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
4 
the basalt flow was deposited.  That is, there are ridges and valleys at the subsurface 
soil/bedrock contact.  The water table is generally shallow, but is also variable due 
to the complex fine-grained soils, course-grained (permeable) soils, and variable 
bedrock surface.  Numerous borehole logs identifying lithology and geotechnical 
properties of the subsurface soils and bedrock were provided to Zonge for this 
investigation by Geolabs. 
The primary objective of this seismic investigation was to characterize the 
variability of the soil deposits.  The secondary objective was to image the soil / 
bedrock interface.  The upper 100 feet of subsurface was the primary depth of 
interest for this investigation.  Additionally, data from this geophysical investigation 
is available for other geotechnical considerations of the HHCTCP project. Resultant 
2D seismic profile data are intended to assess conditions between and below 
existing borings. Therefore, an appropriate seismic method that could work under 
urban, noisy, heavy traffic conditions was required.  Additionally, the method needs 
to have little or no affect due to the presence of groundwater in the soil pore spaces 
or bedrock fractures.  The Refraction MicroTremor (ReMi) field method was 
selected because it satisfied both needs discussed above.  ReMi is a new, but rapidly 
becoming a standard and robust geophysical method for use in urban settings to 
derive the shear-wave velocity to 100 feet (Vs100’) for IBC site classification 
(Louis, 2001, see Appendix A).  ReMi is a one-dimensional (1D) ‘seismic 
sounding’ method that measures passive-surface wave dispersion to determine the 
shear-wave (S-wave) velocity distribution with depth below a surface array of 
standard refraction-type geophones.  The method is gaining acceptance for other 
applications beyond the IBC site classification.  Zonge’s extension of the method is 
to slide the acquisition spread down the line and obtain another 1D sounding at the 
new position.  These soundings are concatenated into a two-dimensional (2D) cross 
section.  Zonge has performed numerous 2D ReMi field programs, which is an 
extension of the more standard 1D Vs100’ field method, where imaging the soils / 
bedrock contact beneath rivers (e.g., saturated soil conditions) or in urban settings 
(e.g., noisy site conditions) prohibited use of typical refraction, reflection, or active 
surface-wave testing.  
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
5 
Data Acquisition 
Instrumentation:  Seismic ReMi data for the August 2008 event were acquired 
along the proposed HHCTCP site using a 24-channel Seismic Source DAQ LinkII 
seismograph. This system represents a 24-bit seismograph that is connected to a 
field laptop via ethernet cable. Analog signals from vertically-oriented geophones 
are collected in the DAQ LinkII seismograph where they are anti-alias filtered, 
converted to a digital signal, transmitted to the laptop computer and then recorded 
on the hard drive. An array of 48 receivers (geophones) was placed on the ground 
along the survey line at 10-foot intervals. The receivers were all low-frequency 4.5-
Hz vertical component, velocity-transducer geophones. A DAQ LinkII seismograph 
has a 24-channel capacity.  The 48-channel receiver array was coupled to a Geostuff 
roll-along box which determines which channels to activate (up to 24) per 
measurement.  For the October 2009 field event two Geometrics Geode 
seismographs were used in the same configuration.  The advantage of this hardware 
setup is that all 48 channels could be recorded simultaneously without use of a roll-
along box. 
Each 1D ReMi sounding was acquired using a 24-channel (or 48 channel) active 
array and a backhoe for a ‘passive’ (non triggered) energy source.  The backhoe was 
located between 100 and 200 feet off the end of the line.  Physical positioning of 
the backhoe was dictated by intersections and coned-off areas.  Multiple records 
were acquired per active receiver array to generate a single sounding.  The active 
array (of 24 or 48 geophones) would increment up, or “roll-along”, by 4 receivers, 
thus providing a single 1D sounding every 40 feet along each line (when recording 
24 channels the roll-along is accomplished with the hardware, when 48 channels 
were recorded the roll-along groups were accomplished in processing).  As will be 
described in the next section, the 1D ReMi sounding data are placed at the middle 
of the 230-foot receiver array.  Because of this, each ReMi line for this project is a 
total of 230 feet longer than the 2D cross-sections presented for each line. That is, 
for each line there were 12 geophones (115 feet) behind and ahead of the first and 
last 1D Vs100 sounding, respectively.  Table 1 identifies each line, the approximate 
stationing, line length, cross-section length, and its reference position.  Each line 
has a different length, location and number of soundings, due to logistical issues for 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
6 
traffic control, or cultural restrictions such as bridge crossings.  No seismic method 
is capable of imaging the subsurface on elevated structures. 
Coverage:  ReMi data were acquired along nine survey lines. The general locations 
for each of the nine lines are shown on Figure 2.  Table 1 lists the ReMi line 
numbers, the approximate stationing, associated line & cross-section lengths, as 
well as a reference position.  The line segments start at the west-most end of the 
survey area, near Pearl City, and continue in a non-continuous fashion to Halawa 
Stream at the southeast end.  Location data in Table 1 indicate there is ‘nearly’ 
continuous line coverage for the survey area, except maybe a major intersection 
where safety issues prevented acquiring data across them.  But in most cases, 
because of the traffic and police control, lines were able to be pushed continuously 
through small intersections, turn-outs, turn-arounds, and retail entrances.  Lines 
were set up along either the median, in the right-most lane of traffic, or on the 
sidewalk, depending on the day of the week and anticipated vehicle traffic load. The 
individual lines were acquired on separate dates that were most appropriate for the 
specific location.  Lines 10 and 11 have a 310 foot overlap in coverage (80 foot on 
cross sections). Geolabs personnel assigned the place for data acquisition daily.  It 
took constant interaction between Zonge and Geolabs personnel to confirm traffic 
control, police escort, backhoe operators, and highway officials for safe and 
reasonable working conditions to be achieved.  Thanks to this combined effort a 
successful and safe field effort was completed in closed traffic lanes.  Through the 
effort of the entire field crew, line coverage varied daily with a minimum line length 
of 960 feet, and a maximum line length of 1,960 feet of data acquisition in a single 
field day.  Typically, about 1,500 to 1,600 of 2D data were acquired daily.  Up to 
2515 feet of data was acquired in a single day in the second round of data 
acquisition, as the 48 channel acquisition set up is faster in the field.

 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc.
