Geolabs provided Zonge with Google™ Earth images showing approximate line 
locations for use during the field work.  The images contained the ReMi lines and 
also the geotechnical boring locations for reference positioning only.  These images 
were used for basic line locations with respect to intersections, buildings, and other 
physical landmarks (e.g., canals and drainage channels). 
ReMi Data:  The following section briefly describes the ReMi method.  Additional 
information is provided in Appendix A – Introduction to the ReMi Method. 
The ReMi method uses the same instrumentation and field layout as a standard 
seismic refraction survey. However, with this seismic method there are no 
predefined source points or any need for timed or ‘triggered’ seismic shots.  ReMi is 
classified as a passive surface-wave technique.  That is, the ReMi method uses 
ambient noise, or vibrational energy that exists at a site without the use of input 
energy from hammers or explosives like those used on refraction and reflection 
seismic surveys, or even multi-channel 
analysis 
of 
surface-wave 
(MASW) 
seismic surveys.  Ambient energy can be 
anything from foot traffic to vehicles, 
construction activities, tidal energy and 
microtremor earthquakes. All of these 
noise sources are present at the HHCTCP 
project site. However, it was clear for this 
project that the primary ambient noise was 
‘along-the-line’ or parallel to the line, 
caused by heavy traffic.  Such noise is not 
the best surface-wave energy to produce 
quality results, as documented by many surface wave experts (e.g., Miller, 2007).  
Because the program used a single line where the receiver array and the (primary) 
ambient energy are parallel, that is the energy travels up and down the line, it was 
decided that an off-line high-amplitude energy source would create more coherent 
surface-wave energy.  As a result of initial field tests conducted at the west end of 
the survey area (i.e., Line 1 in Zonge March 2009 report) it was deemed appropriate 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
9 
to use an active source through the use of a rubber-tired backhoe tamping the 
ground surface with the bucket using varying force.  The backhoe easily generated 
wideband frequency and amplitude surface waves. The inset photo shows the 
backhoe on the far end of an intersection producing surface wave energy into the 
receiver array.  The use of backhoes has been successfully applied on numerous 
‘passive’ wave surveys.  Although it may appear to be an ‘active’ source, there is no 
timing required for the impacts.  The photo also shows the data control car (blue 
van), traffic cones, and police officer which were required to traverse intersections 
like the one shown. 
Either by active noise, ambient energy and other cultural disturbances, these small-
strain vibrations causes surface wave energy to propagate up and down the line (in 
both directions).  These vibrations are the seismic energy that is recorded / 
measured by the ReMi method.  Actual positioning of the active source energy (i.e., 
the backhoe) is not relevant for the ReMi data analysis.  For nearly all the sounding 
locations along the Farrington Highway at least ten (10), unfiltered, 30-second 
ambient and backhoe vibrational energy records were recorded on 24 of the 48 
channel array using a 2 msec sample rate.  In order to roll-through busy 
intersections quickly only five (5) 30-second records were acquired.  These 5 
records proved sufficient during data analysis to provide quality soundings beneath 
these areas.  Also, for quality control purposes, ReMi records were collected with 
and without the backhoe source.  Results from these comparison records will be 
discussed in the results section. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
10 
The illustration below diagrams the ’roll-along’ nature of placing 48 receivers on 
the ground and recording seismic data on 24 active channels.  This set up allows the 
operator to quickly move up the active 24 channels and continue recording data 
while receivers are picked up behind the active array, and laid out ahead of the 
array.  The upper diagram (A) shows just the initial two cable set up with 48 
receivers, while the lower diagram (B) identifies the sounding number and 
location(s) for a long profile.  This diagram illustrates the field set-up used for the 
HHCTCP investigation for the August 2008 field event.  For the October 2009 field 
event the grouping was done in processing as opposed to the field.  It is the lower 
diagram that clearly identifies that the first 115 feet and the last 115 feet of seismic 
line that do not produce subsurface coverage (i.e., soundings) beneath those ends of 
the line(s). 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
11 
Data Processing 
The ReMi method calculates the shear-wave velocity (Vs) of layers and the 
respective depths to interfaces beneath the refraction line as described by Louie 
(2001). The ReMi method is primarily used to calculate shear velocity versus depth 
at a point (1D) in order to differentiate between different soil and rock strata.  The 
method is a surface-wave technique that correlates the Rayleigh-wave velocity with 
the subsurface shear-wave velocity. 
Noise records (including backhoe hits) collected at this site were processed using 
the SeisOpt® ReMi™ software, © Optim LLC, 2005 (Louie, 2001). The ReMi 
technique is based on two fundamental concepts. The first is that seismic-refraction 
recording equipment can effectively record surface waves at frequencies as low as 2 
Hz with the use of low-frequency geophones. The second idea is that a simple, two-
dimensional slowness-frequency (p-f) transform of a microtremor record can 
separate Rayleigh surface waves from other seismic arrivals.  This separation allows 
recognition of the true phase velocity and discriminates against energy propagating 
in other modes without dispersion.  The advantages of ReMi from a seismic 
surveying point of view are several, including the following: It requires only 
standard refraction instrumentation; it requires no triggered source of wave energy; 
and, it will work best in seismically noisy urban settings. Traffic, construction, and 
wind responses of trees, buildings, and utility standards provide the surface wave 
energy (“source”) for the recordings. Augmentation of the ambient sources assured 
in-line propagated was the dominant energy recorded. 
There are four processing steps to derive the 2D ReMi Vs profiles: 
Step 1: Create a velocity spectrum or slowness (p) versus frequency (f) curve (i.e., a 
p-f plot) from the noise records - The distinctive slope of dispersive surface waves 
is an advantage of the p-f analysis; body waves and airwaves cannot have such a 
slope. Even if most of the energy in a seismic record is in a phase other than 
Rayleigh waves, the p-f analysis will identify the dispersion of the surface waves.  
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
12 
Step 2: Pick the Rayleigh-wave dispersion - Picking is done along a lowest p-f 
envelope bounding the dispersed energy appearing on the p-f image. The picks thus 
discriminate against higher apparent velocities present when ‘noise’ impacts the 
linear array from off-line or out-of-line directions. Picking a surface-wave 
dispersion curve along an envelope of the lowest phase velocities at each frequency 
has a further desirable effect. Because higher-mode Rayleigh waves have phase 
velocities above those of the fundamental mode, the refraction microtremor 
technique preferentially yields the fundamental-mode surface wave phase velocities.  
Step 3: Generate1D shear wave velocity models - The ReMi method interactively 
forward-models the normal-mode dispersion data picked from the p-f images with a 
computer algorithm adapted from Saito (1979, 1988) by Yuehua Zeng(1992).  This 
algorithm produces results identical to those of the forward-modeling computer 
codes used by Iwata et al. (1998), and by Xia et al. (1999) within their inversion 
procedure.  The modeling iterates on phase velocity at each period (frequency).  The 
analysis approach and the propagation properties of surface waves allow velocity 
reversals (low Vs layers at depth) to be modeled successfully. 
Step 4: Generate 2D shear wave profiles – Once a series of 1D Vs soundings have 
been modeled, the data are entered into a contouring or smoothing algorithm that 
produces a 2D smooth-model from the 1D inverted models / soundings.  The 2D 
profile represents a cross-section of the subsurface with soundings spaced at 40 feet 
(for this project).  Initially an ‘unsmoothed’ profile is generated, which only plots 
the 1D sounding without any smoothing operation applied, then the smoothing is 
applied appropriately for the anticipated geologic setting.  A concerted effort is 
taken during Step 4 to ensure that any geologic or geotechnical data available are 
analyzed and integrated with the final processing step. Soil stiffness (e.g., relative 
density from blow counts) and rock competency (from drill core RQD), and depth 
that bedrock was encountered are important parameters for modeling (1D – Step 3) 
and generating 2D smooth ReMi profiles. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
13 
A few of these steps are diagrammatically shown on the figure below, purely for 
illustrative purpose.  The figure is adopted from a Kansas Geologic Survey 
publication that refers to MASW type of surface-wave data, whereas this illustration 
has been modified to show noise records and associated passive-wave soundings 
(referred to as “X” positions). 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
14 
Results & Interpretations 
Results from the 2D ReMi analysis for Lines 6 through 14 are presented on Figures 
3 through 17. These figures were generated in a manner that allows comparison 
from line to line; that is, each figure uses the same color (velocity) scale.  All lines 
have the same vertical exaggeration.  Each figure presents up to 760 line feet of 
ReMi cross-section; therefore, the longer lines required additional pages and have 
more than one figure to accommodate the additional line length (see Table 1).  
Table 1 presents the line lengths and respective stationing shown on Figures 3 
through 17.  Depth of investigation was a minimum of 120 ft.  The figures are 11 x 
17 inch tabloid plates.  Stationing and elevation, as shown on each figure, were 
taken from the Track Alignment Plan and Profile drawings (dated June16 2009) 
provided to Zonge, and should be considered approximate. Elevations for lines 13 
and 14 were taken from the topographic map (SJ-topo480.dwg) provided to Zonge 
in November 2009, and are also approximate. 
Where geotechnical borings were located along the 2D ReMi lines, their locations 
have been posted on the figures for integration of material property data with the 
ReMi shear-wave velocity results.  These boring data were provided by Geolabs.  
Stationing of the boreholes was provided by Geolabs personnel.  Two velocity 
contours have been placed on the cross-sections: 1) a 600 feet per second (ft/s) 
contour shown in yellow which represents an interpreted transition from soft / loose 
to stiff / dense soil deposits; and, 2) a 2,000 ft/s contour (2,300 ft/s for lines 13 and 
14) shown in black that is interpreted to best represent the sharp velocity gradient 
between soils and bedrock.  The basaltic bedrock surface, as described on Geolabs 
boring logs, shows a wide variation in the degree of weathering (e.g., slightly to 
extremely weathered), as well as a substantial difference in the fracture density 
(e.g., moderately to highly fractured).  Based on close inspection of the boring logs 
at the respective locations along the ReMi profiles, and based on experience, actual 
bedrock shear-wave velocity likely ranges between about 1,800 and 2,400 ft/s at 
this project site. Fracturing significantly decreases the compressional- or shear-
wave velocity of a rock formation.  
As described previously, and discussed in greater detail in Appendix A, the ReMi 
seismic technique is a seismic method that averages the subsurface bulk properties 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
15 
beneath a 24-channel, 230-foot, receiver array.  Each sounding represents that bulk 
or average shear-wave velocity.  The cross-sections shown on Figure 3 through 15 
are the best representation of the lateral and vertical variation of soft soils, and the 
depth to- and geometry of- the basalt contact. These seismic data are the best 
interpretation of the material properties between, below and beyond geotechnical 
drill hole control.  The ReMi data indicate significant variation and relief on the 
bedrock surface, as anticipated, and there is good correlation between boring logs 
and bedrock depths as interpreted by 2D seismic profiling. 
“Soft soils” are defined, in this investigation, as undifferentiated soil deposits that 
have a shear-wave velocity of less than 600 feet/second (ft/s). Soils with a shear-
wave velocity of less than 600 ft/s are classified as Class E - soft soils, according to 
the 2006 International Building Code.  Soils with shear-wave velocities between 
600 ft/s and 1,200 ft/s are classified as Class D - stiff soils.  Based on the yellow 
contour line (600 ft/s) shown on Figures 3 though 15, the thickness of soft soils 
varies considerably across the project area.  On the west end (i.e., Line 6) there are 
virtually no soft soils, but by the west end of Line 8 (~Station 853+86) the thickness 
of soft soil goes to slightly over 30 feet bgs.  Beneath Line 9 the soft soils are about 
80-feet thick (Station 867+50 to 868+30).  From approximately station 872+07 to 
878+75 on line 10 the soft soils are over 50 feet thick, thinning abruptly at station 
879+00 to approximately ten feet in thickness until  pinching out at station 881+00. 
 Little or no soft soils occur between stations 881+00 and 889+00. From station 
889+00 the thickness of soft soils increases gradually to a maximum of near 80 feet 
at station 914+50 on line 12. The thickness of soft soils decreases relative sharply 
and goes to zero at station 918+00 on line 12 and no soft soils are encountered in 
the last 135 feet of the line 12.  Approximately 20 feet of soft soils were 
encountered from 945+00 to 950+00 on Line 13 near Aiea Stream. 
At the beginning of the survey on Line 6 at station 800+05 the ReMi model shows 
the 2000 ft/sec velocity contour (interpreted to be the top of basalt) at a depth of 
~45 feet below ground surface (bgs).  Bedrock appears to be flat until station 
803+50 and then rises ~15 feet in fifty feet to ~35 feet bgs.  At boring B-229 
(805+40) bedrock was encountered 40 feet bgs.  The 2000 ft/sec contour at that 
station is at ~ 36 feet bgs, the modeled velocity at 40 ft bgs is ~2400 ft/sec. Along 
the remainder of Line 6 the 2000 ft/sec velocity contour shows a gradual undulation 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
16 
of bedrock between 25-40 feet bgs.  At station 811+60 boring B-230 encountered 
top of basalt at 65 feet.  The 2000 ft/sec contour at that station is at ~ 40 feet bgs, 
the modeled velocity at 65 ft bgs is ~2900 ft/sec.  At 40 feet bgs on the boring log 
shows very dense sandy gravel composed of weathered basalt with penetration 
resistance of over 50 blow counts per foot (samples of this gravel above 40 feet bgs 
show blow counts below 50 blows/foot).   
The Line 7 2000 ft/sec velocity contour shows bedrock depth ranging from ~10-35 
feet bgs, with the deepest bedrock at station 831+00. Shallow bedrock is 
encountered at stations 830+00, 832+50 and 836+00.  At station 828+10 boring B-
233 encountered top of basalt at 30.5 feet.  The 2000 ft/sec contour at that station is 
at ~ 25 feet bgs, the modeled velocity at 30.5 ft bgs is ~3500 ft/sec.  At 25 feet on 
the boring log for B-233 is a contact between a clayey silt and sandy gravel 
(designated Saprolite on log).  At station 833+30 boring B-234 encountered top of 
basalt at 5 feet bgs.  The 2000 ft/sec contour at that station is at ~ 22 feet bgs, the 
modeled velocity at 5 ft bgs is ~1500 ft/sec. At 22 ft bgs on the boring log the basalt 
grades from highly weathered, medium hard to moderately weathered very hard. 
 At the beginning of Line 8, station 853+86, the model shows bedrock at ~35 feet 
bgs.  Bedrock deepens to a maximum depth of ~55 feet bgs at station 857+00, rises 
to a depth of ~25 feet bgs at station 859+50. Bedrock remains fairly level until 
station 862+00, and then drops ~20 feet in ~100 feet and is at 40 feet bgs until the 
end of the line.  At station 862+30 boring CPT-116 encountered top of basalt at 28 
feet bgs.  The 2000 ft/sec contour at that station is at ~ 30 feet bgs, the modeled 
velocity at 28 ft bgs is ~1700 ft/sec.   
Line 9 is a short cross section due to obstructions in the field.  In this section 
bedrock was modeled very deep at ~ 90 feet.  This interpretation confirmed by the 
boring B-236 which encountered top of basalt at 94.5 feet bgs.   
Lines 10 and 11 represent 2800 feet of continuous coverage, which generated 2570 
feet of cross section.  There is enough overlap on the lines to create 80 feet of 
overlap in the sections.  At station 886+50 were the two lines developed 
independent solutions from separate soundings the two bedrock picks were within 3 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
17 
feet.  From the beginning of line 10 at station 872+07 to station 883+00 bedrock is 
relatively level experiencing a slight rise from almost 90 feet bgs to ~60 feet bgs. 
From station 883+00 to 887+00 there is what appears to be a knob of bedrock that 
raises to ~25 feet bgs.  The rest of the line shows a gradual increase in depth (except 
between stations 888+50 to 891+00 where there is a slight rise) to a depth of ~60 
feet bgs.  At station 875+00 boring B-237 encountered top of basalt at 92.5 feet bgs. 
 The 2000 ft/sec contour at that station is at ~ 86 feet bgs, the modeled velocity at

encountered top of basalt at 57.5 feet bgs.  The 2000 ft/sec contour at that station is 
at ~42 feet bgs, the modeled velocity at 57 ft bgs is ~2800 ft/sec.   Blow count on 
this boring took a sharp rise at 46 feet bgs when very hard clayey silt with cobbles 
was encountered.   
Line 12 shows a gradual decrease in depth from the beginning of the line at station 
912+15 (~110 feet bgs) to station 919+00 (~67 feet bgs).  This decreasing depth 
continues to the end of the line at 926+00 where it reaches 45 feet bgs. 
At a line-location of 415 feet for Line 13 (see Figure 3), the 2D ReMi model shows 
the 2,300 ft/sec velocity contour at an elevation of approximately -95 feet.  Here, 
borehole data from B-120 indicates high penetration resistances at this depth, and 
the 2,300 ft/sec contour is interpreted to be indicative of very stiff/hard sediments in 
the vicinity of this borehole.  Further along Line 13, at a line location of 830 feet, 
the 2,300 ft/sec contour correlates with the top of basaltic rock encountered in 
boring B-434.  This contour indicates that very stiff/hard sediments and/or basaltic 
rock are present from approximately 80 to 130 feet bgs along all of Line 13, where 
this contour generally deepens in the up-station direction (towards Line 14). 
The Line 14 2,300 ft/sec velocity contour continues to deepen in the up-station 
direction as it approaches the Halawa Stream.  The thick low velocities near the 
southwest end of Line 14 correlate well with the findings of borehole B-18 located 
just beyond the southwest-end of the model, where soft sediments with low 
penetration resistances were encountered throughout the entire boring (terminated at 
168 feet bgs).  At a line-location of ~1,400 feet for Line 14 (see Figure 4), the 2D 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
18 
ReMi model shows the 2,300 ft/sec velocity contour drop off beyond the depth of 
investigation for this survey.  This is likely a processing artifact for this region of 
line 14, as the 2,300 ft/sec contour continues at approximately the same elevation 
just on the other side of the Halawa Stream along Line 15 (see Figure 5).  
Therefore, it is believed that very stiff/hard sediments and associated higher 
penetration resistances exist just beyond the termination depth of borehole B-18.   
Conclusions 
The acquisition of 2D seismic data along a portion of the HHCTCP alignment 
provided very good imaging of the overburden soils and the soil/bedrock contact.  
The ReMi method was selected because of its ability to acquire seismic data in 
(very) noisy urban environments and to distinguish between soft soils, stiffer soils 
and bedrock based on their shear-wave velocity.  The ReMi passive surface-wave 
method is not affected by the presence of a shallow water table, which was 
important as much of the survey area was very near the coast.   
This geophysical survey met the primary project objective - delineating areas of soft 
soils. This is corroborated by geotechnical boring data. Correlation with geologic 
logs for thick soft soil zones  
and depth to bedrock was very good.  The ReMi data indicate areas of thick (>60 
feet) material with shear wave velocities less than 600 ft/s, interpreted to be soft 
soils. 
There was general agreement between the borehole data and geophysical data to 
reasonably state that the secondary survey objective - image the soil/bedrock 
contact - was achieved.  Based on boring logs from the holes along ReMi Lines 6 
through 12, bedrock conditions are not expected to be uniform or homogenous 
within the survey area.  As such, some variability of the shear-wave velocity is 
expected to exist in the transition zone from stiff soils to weathered bedrock to 
competent bedrock.  The choice of a 2,000 ft/s shear-wave velocity contour, shown 
on each cross-section, was used to depict the interpreted bedrock velocity.  This 
choice was based on the upper range Vs values for stiff soils transitioning to soft 
rock. ReMi results collected adjacent to geotechnical borings indicate that the 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
 Project #08126/09138 Geolabs, Inc. 
19 
soil/basalt contact depths correlate very well with modeled shear-wave velocities 
between 1,800 ft/s and 2,400 ft/s.  Shear wave velocities of 1,800 ft/s in a rock mass 
would generally indicate intensely weathered or highly fractured rock; and, shear 
wave velocities of 2,400 ft/s or greater generally indicates competent bedrock. For 
the second round of data processing (Lines 13 and 14) 2,300 ft/s was selected for 
the bedrock interface velocity as this velocity provided the best fit to the borehole 
logs. This velocity although slightly higher than that of the first round is still in the 
range of 1,800-2400 ft/s.

APPENDIX A 
METHOD BRIEF: 
AN INTRODUCTION TO 
THE REFRACTION MICROTREMOR 
SURVEY METHOD 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Zonge Geosciences, Inc. 
1990 S. Garrison Street, Suite #2 
Lakewood, CO  80227 
Phone: (720) 962-4444 
INTRODUCTION TO THE REFRACTION MICROTREMOR METHOD – ReMiTM 
OBJECTIVE: 
The objective of the geophysical survey is to determine in-situ shear-wave (S-wave) 
velocities of the overburden soil deposits and the bedrock (if encountered) to arrive at a 
“Vs30”; that is, the average shear-wave velocity to 30 meters (100 feet).  The Vs data 
are to be acquired for site classification purposes as prescribed in Section 1615.1.1 of 
IBC 2000. The Refraction Microtremor (ReMi) method does not require the use of a 
borehole or active seismic sources (impact hammers, blasting, etc.). 
FIELD METHODS: 
To meet the objective, Zonge GeoSciences (Zonge) recommends conducting a set of 
passive surface-wave seismic tests at the project site, using the new technology of 
Refraction Microtremor – ReMi.  The proposed work is accomplished in noisy urban 
environments using a specialized array of surface geophones (low frequency phones) 
and measuring the ambient seismic vibrations.  The field setup involves laying out a 100-
meter line with 12 geophones spaced 8m apart (~300 ft and ~26 ft, respectively).  A 
seismograph is set to record data over a 30 second time interval, which is repeated 
several times (as many as 10) for signal processing purposes.  These data are 
processed to develop a Vs versus depth curve to 30 meters (100 feet).  
The technology for ReMi is based on the useful Spectral Analysis of Surface (SASW), 
and more recently the Multi-channel Analysis of Surface Waves (MASW) methods.  
Surface waves comprise about 80% of the energy from a hammer blows, weight drops 
or surface vibrations caused by ambient energy (e.g., vehicles, construction, drilling and 
sampling, etc.).  The picture below illustrates the field setup for MASW.  SASW uses 
only 4 receivers, ReMi uses 12 receivers, but they both produce a 1D shear wave 
velocity (Vs) curve.  Low velocity layers at depth can be detected with either the SASW, 
MASW, or ReMi techniques.  The only significant difference between SASW or MASW 
and ReMi seismic testing is that no active sources are required for ReMi – it monitors the 
‘passive’ energy in the ground, or microtremors. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Zonge Geosciences, Inc. 
1990 S. Garrison Street, Suite #2 
Lakewood, CO  80227 
Phone: (720) 962-4444 
Using ReMi in a similar fashion, and combining the data acquisition procedures with 
some MASW-style measurements (or active energy sources), provides a profile of Vs.  
For projects where Vs30 is the only requirement, only one array set up is required for 
ReMi.  However, it is recommended by most geotechnical engineers that multiple set-
ups be placed around the proposed structure(s) to determine if lateral variability of the 
soil thickness or stiffness must be taken into account for the design.   
The ReMi method has proved to be extremely useful in urban settings where standard 
surface seismic methods fail, and borehole methods (e.g., downhole or crosshole 
seismic) can have data that are difficult to 
interpret in the upper 10 meters (20-30 
feet). The picture to the left shows the 
ReMi system deployed along 14th Street in 
downtown Denver, Colorado.  A shear-
wave profile is presented from the ReMi 
field measurements, and a table of 
velocities for the upper 30 meters.  The 
Excel sheet containing the velocities 
automatically computes the average over 
the upper 30 meters (Vs30).  Depending 
on the site conditions and ambient noise 
sources nearby, this ReMi test method 
can determine Vs to much greater depths, 
as evidenced in Reno by Dr. J. Louie (2001), from the University of Nevada-Reno.  The 
ReMi approach for Vs30 has been developed by Optim, a adjunct professional 
organization to the University at Reno (Mackay School of Mines).  The ReMi software 
uses an algorithm to compute the dispersion curve form the ambient noise 
measurements and forward model the data to a Vs curve.  ReMi Vs measurements have 
been consistently shown to be within 10% or less of standard downhole or crosshole 
measurements.  See example below (Bureau of Reclamation 2002).  Additionally, Zonge 
has collected ReMi and downhole seismic on the same site and shown less than 10 
percent difference in layer thickness, shear-wave velocity, and computed Vs30. 
Zonge Geosciences has completed over 50 ReMi surveys in the front-range corridor of 
Colorado in the past 2 ½ years.  The method has been effective for determining Vs30 at 
quite sites (where we need to impart energy into the ground), at construction sites, and 
in suburban/residential settings.

A P P E N D I X   E 
Field Point Load Testing 
Field point load testing was performed on selected rock cores obtained during 
our field exploration program in general accordance with ASTM D 5731 – Determination 
of the Point Load Strength Index of Rock and Application to Rock Strength 
Classifications.   
The point load test measures the Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) of rock 
specimens in the form of either core (the diametral and axial tests), cut blocks (the block 
test), or irregular lumps (the irregular lump test). The rock specimens are broken by 
application of concentrated load through a pair of spherically truncated, conical platens. 
For this project, Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) were determined from one hundred 
and twenty-four (124) diametral and axial tests performed on selected rock cores 
retrieved at B-115 through B-118, B-226, B-238, and B-434 through B-436 during our 
field exploration program. Tests with fracture surface passing through only one loading 
point were rejected and not reported in this appendix. 
The field point load tests were performed using a Model 77-0110 Point Load Test 
Apparatus manufactured by ELE International and provided by PB Americas, Inc. The 
apparatus consists of a rigid frame, two point load platens, a hydraulically activated ram 
with pressure gauge and a device for measuring the distance between the loading 
points. The pressure gauge is of the type in which the failure pressure can be recorded. 
The test involves compressing a rock sample between conical steel plates until failure 
occurs. The following data were recorded from which the Uncorrected Point Load Strength 
Index (Is) of the specimen was determined: 
Is  = P / De
2, MPa 
where: 
P = failure load, N, 
De = equivalent core diameter, mm, and is given by: 
De
2  = D2 for diametral core tests without penetration, mm2 
or 
De
2  = 4A/ for axial, block, and lump tests, mm2 
where: 
A = WD = minimum cross-sectional area of a plane through the platen contact 
points  
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Appendix E 
Field Point Load Testing 
W.O. 6000-20   
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2010     Page E-2 
Hawaii ● California 
The Point Load Strength Index (Is(50)) then is obtained by applying a 
“Size Correction Factor F” to the Uncorrected Point Load Strength Index (Is): 
Is(50) = F * Is  
where: 
F = (De/50)0.45 
The estimated uniaxial compressive strength (c) may be obtained by the 
following formula: 
c = K * Is(D) 
where: 
c  = uniaxial compressive strength, MPa 
K    = index to strength conversion factor that depends on site-specific correlation 
between c and Is(D), MPa 
Is(D) = corrected point load strength index at a given (D) 
The field data collected and the determination of the Point Load Strength Index 
(Is(50)) and the estimated uniaxial compressive strength (c) are presented on Plates E-1 
through E-3.

A P P E N D I X   F 
Groundwater Level Monitoring 
Groundwater levels were measured during advancement of the boreholes drilled 
for the project at the times shown on the Logs of Borings presented in Appendix A and 
summarized in Table F-1. The artesian head measurements indicated in B-117 and 
B-119 were based on the height of the water level inside the PQ casing that extended 
above the existing pavement surface. 
In addition, groundwater monitoring points were attempted at three boring 
locations, including B-117(P), B-120, and B-229(P), to monitor seasonal fluctuations and 
to measure groundwater gradients along the project segment under consideration in this 
report. It should be noted that establishment of a groundwater monitoring point was 
attempted at B-120; however, the three piezometer cables placed inside the borehole 
were inadvertently severed during installation. The boring locations where these 
groundwater monitoring points are attempted and/or established are shown in the 
following table and on the Boring Location Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.8. 
GROUNDWATER MONITORING POINTS

+41.7 
A total of four vibrating wire piezometers, Model 4500S manufactured by Geokon, 
Inc., were installed at selected depths within borings B-117 and B-229 to monitor the 
groundwater levels on a near-continuous basis using a Model LC-2 datalogger 
manufactured by Geokon, Inc. Catalog sheets of the vibrating wire piezometer and 
datalogger are presented in Figures F-1 and F-2 for illustration purposes only. The actual 
elevations of the vibrating wire piezometers are reflected on the installation details 
prepared for each monitoring point as shown on Plates F-1.1 and F-2.1. 
Prior to the installation, the filter stone is saturated by placing the piezometer under 
water for a minimum of 12 hours and transported to the field in a submerged condition. In 
addition, zero readings were obtained in the field prior to the installation of each 
piezometer, which provided a baseline reference under field conditions and served as an 
acceptance test – a verification that the piezometer is working properly. The piezometers 
are embedded in a filter sand pack and sealed at the top and bottom with bentonite to 
isolate the piezometer at the specific stratum where groundwater level measurements are 
desired. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Appendix F 
Groundwater Level Monitoring 
W.O. 6000-20

FEBRUARY 2010     Page F-2 
Hawaii ● California 
The groundwater level data were downloaded in the field to a laptop computer on 
a regular basis and processed in our office. It should be noted that there are some 
periods where the data acquisition lost some of the data collected resulting in blanks in 
the data set. However, it does not appear that there are significant anomalies in the 
groundwater levels to raise concerns based on the other available groundwater level 
monitoring data. Typical groundwater level data obtained by the piezometers are shown 
on Table F-2. Graphic presentations of the groundwater level data collected are provided 
on Plates F-1.2 through F-2.4.

VIBRATING WIRE PIEZOMETERS 
Standard Piezometers 
Model 4500S(H), 4500AL(V) 
The Model 4500 Standard Piezometer is designed to measure fluid
pressures such as ground water elevations and pore pressures when
buried directly in embankments, fills, etc. Also for installation inside
boreholes, observation wells and standard (>19 mm diameter)
piezometer riser pipe. The Model 4500SH is designed with a heavy
duty housing for pressures that exceed 3 MPa. The Model 4500AL is
designed for low-pressure ranges. The vented version (ALV) provides
automatic 
compensation 
for 
barometric 
pressure 
changes.
Thermistors are included to measure temperatures.* 
Specifications 
4500S 
4500SH 
4500AL(V) 
Standard Ranges 
350, 700 kPa; 
1, 2, 3 MPa 
5, 7.5 MPa 
70, 170 kPa 
Over Range 
2 × rated pressure 
Resolution 
0.025% F.S. (minimum) 
Accuracy 
±0.1% F.S. 
Linearity¹ 
<0.5% F.S. (±0.1% F.S. optional) 
Temperature Range² 
−20°C to +80°C 
Length × Diameter 
133 × 19.1 
mm 
194 × 25.4 
mm 
133 × 25.4 
mm 
¹±0.1% F.S. option available in some ranges. 
²High t
emperature versions available on request.

FIGURE F-1 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
SINGLE-CHANNEL DATALOGGER 
Single-Channel Dataloggers 
Model 8002-1, 8002-1A (LC-2, LC-2A) 
The Model 8002-1 (LC-2) and Model 8002-1A (LC-2A) are
stand-alone, single-channel dataloggers, designed to read both the
vibrating wire element and the integral 
thermistor 
of any
Geokon vibrating wire sensor. The LC-2 offers an internal hard wired
transducer connection and the LC-2A provides an optional 10-pin
transducer connector for rapid sensor connection. The dataloggers
are housed inside a Fiberglass Nema 4X enclosure, are very robust,
weather-proof and particularly well-suited to operation in harsh
environments. Power is supplied by two easily accessible ‘D’ cells or
by solar panel for remote long-term applications. 16,000 arrays can
be stored (8,000 arrays when used with LogWare) before the oldest
data is over-written. Strings of LC-2 dataloggers can be daisy
chained using the RS-485 interface, and versions are available with
radios and modems to provide remote readout capabilities. Complete
with Windows® based, user-friendly software. 
Specifications 
Measurement Accuracy 
0.05% F.S. (450-4000Hz) 
Measurement 
Resolution 
1 part in 20,000 
Program Memory 
24K FLASH 
Data Memory 
320K EEPROM 
Temperature Range 
−40°C to +60°C 
L × W × H 
122 × 120 × 91 mm

APPENDIX G 
Laboratory Testing 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
GEOLABS, INC. 
Hawaii ● California 
Page Intentionally Left Blank for Double-Sided Printing 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2010     Page G-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   G 
Laboratory Testing 
Samples recovered from our field exploration program performed for the 
Kamehameha Highway Segment extending from Pearl City to Aiea of the HHCTCP 
were visually examined and classified in our laboratory in Honolulu, Hawaii. Selected 
samples were tested to evaluate the physical characteristics and engineering properties 
of the soils encountered. The laboratory testing program was formulated with the 
emphasis on evaluating the moisture, density, plasticity, gradation, compressibility, 
shear strength, corrosivity, and organic content of the soils encountered. 
The laboratory tests performed included determination of dry density and natural 
moisture 
content, 
Atterberg 
Limits, 
sieve 
analysis, 
hydrometer, 
unconfined 
compression, triaxial compression, direct shear, consolidation, corrosion, and organic 
content tests. Results of the tests performed are summarized in Tables G-1.1 through 
G-2.2 and are presented on Plates G-1 through G-80. Detailed descriptions of the 
various tests performed for this exploration are presented below. 
Moisture and Density 
Moisture content and dry density tests were performed on selected soil samples 
to aid in defining the interface between the various soil layers encountered and 
correlating the layers between test borings. The moisture content tests were performed 
in general accordance with ASTM D 2216. The test results are summarized in 
Tables G-1.1 through G-1.17 and are presented on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths. 
Atterberg Limits 
Seventy (70) Atterberg Limits tests were performed on selected soil samples to 
evaluate the liquid limit (LL), plastic limit (PL), and plasticity index (PI), as an aid in 
classifying the soils. The Atterberg Limits tests were performed in general accordance 
with ASTM D 4318. The test results are summarized on the Logs of Borings at the 
appropriate sample depths and in Tables G-1.1 through G-1.17. Graphic presentations 
of the test results are provided on Plates G-1 through G-7. 
Sieve Analysis and Hydrometer 
To provide information on the particle size distribution of the soils encountered, 
thirty-five (35) Sieve Analysis tests including four (4) hydrometer tests were performed 
to evaluate the gradation characteristics and to aid in soil classification. The tests were 
performed in accordance with ASTM C 136 and D 422. Graphic presentation of the test 
results are provided on Plates G-8 through G-14. Gravel, sand, and fines fraction of the 
samples tested are also included in Table G-1. In addition, three (3) tests were 
conducted on selected soil samples to evaluate the amount of fines content. The tests 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Appendix G 
Laboratory Testing 
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2010     Page G-2 
Hawaii ● California 
were performed in accordance with ASTM D 1140. The test results are summarized on 
Plate G-15. 
Unconfined Compression – Soil Samples 
To evaluate the unconfined compressive strength of the on-site clayey soils, two 
(2) Unconfined Compression tests were performed on selected in-situ samples in 
accordance with ASTM D 2166.  The test results are shown on the Logs of Borings at 
the appropriate sample depths and summarized on Table G-2. Individual stress-strain 
curves of the unconfined compression tests are presented on Plates G-16 and G-17. 
Unconfined Compression – Rock Cores 
Sixty-eight (68) Unconfined Compression tests (ASTM D 7012 Method C) were 
performed on selected rock cores to evaluate their unconfined compressive strength of 
the rock formation encountered. Unconfined compression test results are summarized 
on Plates G-18 through G-21. 
Unconsolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the clayey soils, forty-two 
(42) unconsolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression tests were performed on selected 
in-situ soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 2850.  The approximate in-situ effective 
overburden pressure was used as the applied confining pressure for the “undisturbed” 
samples. The test results and the stress-strain curves are presented on Plates G-22 
through G-63. 
Consolidated-Undrained Triaxial Compression 
To evaluate the undrained shear strength of the soft clayey soils, one 
(1) Consolidated Undrained Triaxial Compression test was performed on a selected 
in-situ soil sample in accordance with ASTM D 4767. The test results and the 
stress-strain curves at different confining pressures are presented on Plate G-64. 
Direct Shear 
To 
evaluate 
the 
shear 
strength 
characteristics 
of 
the 
in-situ 
soils, 
five (5) strain-controlled, consolidated-drained Direct Shear tests were performed on the 
in-situ soil samples in accordance with ASTM D 3080. The test results are presented on 
Plates G-65 through G-69. 
Consolidation 
Eight (8) Consolidation tests were performed on selected soil samples to 
evaluate the compressibility characteristics of the on-site soils. These tests were 
performed in accordance with ASTM D 2435, and the test results are plotted on 
Plates G-70 through G-77. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
Appendix G 
Laboratory Testing 
W.O. 6000-20 
GEOLABS, INC. 
FEBRUARY 2010     Page G-3 
Hawaii ● California 
Corrosion Test 
Twenty-five (25) sets of pH (ASTM G51) and minimum resistivity (ASTM G57) 
tests were performed on selected soil samples obtained from our field exploration by 
our office. In addition, twenty-four (24) sets of chloride (EPA 300) and sulfate (EPA 300) 
content tests were performed by TestAmerica, Inc. on selected soil samples obtained 
from our field exploration. The test results are presented on Plates G-78 and G-79. 
Organic Content 
One (1) organic content test (ASTM D 2974) was performed on a selected soil 
sample to evaluate the amount of organic matter in the sample. The test results are 
presented on Plate G-80.

FEBRUARY 2010     Page I-1 
Hawaii ● California 
A P P E N D I X   I 
Selected Data from 1990 & 1991 Geotechnical Engineering Explorations 
A geotechnical engineering exploration was conducted by Geolabs-Hawaii in 
1991 for the Honolulu Rapid Transit Development Project. Selected boring logs and 
laboratory test data from the 1991 study that may be applicable to this project are 
included in this appendix for information and reference purposes. The selected 
subsurface information included 15 borings, designated as B-4 through B-18, at the 
approximate locations shown on the Boring Location Plans, Plates 3.1 through 3.8. 
In addition, a geotechnical and geological reconnaissance was conducted by 
Geolabs-Hawaii in 1990 for the Honolulu Rapid Transit System project.  A selected 
boring log from the 1990 study that may be applicable to this project also is included in 
this appendix for information and reference purposes.  The selected subsurface 
information included on boring, designated as Boring A, drilled at the approximate 
location shown on the Boring Location Plan, Plate 3.4.  
Please note the boring locations for these sixteen borings were not surveyed. 
KHS GDR 02/16/2010
GEOLABS, INC. 
Hawaii ● California
